10.1016/j.jmp.2008.08.003
Multinomial random variables form the cornerstone of categorical data analysis within the social sciences. Most visibly, investigators use contingency tables to analyze frequencies of categorical data ( Agresti, 1996 ). Multinomial random variables are widely used by social scientists in related contexts, such as modeling individual preference/choice ( Iverson & Falmagne, 1985; Myung, Karabatsos, & Iverson, 2005; Tversky, 1969 ) and social choice ( Regenwetter, Grofman, Marley, & Tsetlin, 2006 ). Additionally, psychologists commonly model various cognitive processes with multinomial random variables under a multinomial processing tree framework ( Riefer & Batchelder, 1988 ). See  Batchelder and Riefer (1999)  for a review of similar applications within psychology.

Investigators typically form testable hypotheses by placing restrictions on the permissible parameter space of the statistical model. The two most common restrictions for multinomial models are either to set all parameters equal, (tests for categorical equality), or to assign prescribed values for each parameter, (tests of observed versus expected values). However, there are many interesting models that can take the form of  inequality  restrictions. Suppose an investigator wants to test whether the number of patients with a particular symptom decreases with increasing dosage levels of a drug. This hypothesis could be tested by considering a contingency table with the probability parameters of the categories ordered in a decreasing fashion, i.e., the probability of a patient having the symptom with a single drug dose is greater than the probability of a patient having the symptom with two drug doses, and so on. The investigator can make more meaningful inferences when performing a goodness-of-fit test on this constrained model than they could if they simply tested whether all drug levels have equal probability of having a symptomatic patient. A constrained inference model also provides more statistical power than attempting to perform an equivalent inference with a series of simple pairwise tests (each of which may require a Bonferroni correction at a loss of power).

Inequality-restricted models are infrequently used in the social sciences ( Iverson, 2006 ). Aside from a lack of awareness, two major complications when evaluating these models using frequentist methodology contribute to their relatively rare use. First, we need numerical optimization because typically, the maximum-likelihood estimator does not have a closed-form solution. Second, due to the inequality constraints, the maximum likelihood estimator will often lie on the boundary of the permissible parameter space. Under this condition, the likelihood ratio test statistic no longer follows a simple  
                         
                            
                               χ 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       distribution asymptotically ( Chant, 1974; Chernoff, 1954; Robertson, Wright, & Dykstra, 1988; Shapiro, 1985; Wilks, 1938 ).

Previous work on the topic has considered maximum likelihood estimation and inference on multinomial random variables under a wide variety of constraints ( Dykstra & Robertson, 1982; El Barmi & Dykstra, 1995; Robertson & Wright, 1981; Silvapulle & Sen, 2005 ). The current article summarizes this work within the context of linear inequality constraints, and generalizes existing results by considering the case of jointly independent multinomial random variables of varying categorical size, e.g., a collection of jointly independent binomial and trinomial random variables. The second half of this article presents the methodology as a means of evaluating properly reformulated measurement axioms. As shown in the example section, this framework allows the testing of binary, ternary, and multiple/subset choice under various inequality constraints, e.g., the well-known Block–Marschak inequalities.

The analysis of inequality-constrained multinomial models has also been approached from multiple Bayesian perspectives.  Myung et al. (2005)  have developed a general Bayesian methodology of constrained inference for multinomial models, specifically applying their work to the evaluation of properly reformulated measurement and decision axioms. The current article can be considered as the “frequentist” companion to this methodology.  Chechile (1998, 2004)  presented a population parameter mapping (PPM) methodology that analyzes multinomial models under a variety of constraints. The PPM methodology circumvents many of the traditional obstacles found in the frequentist analysis through the use of Bayesian assumptions, and has been successfully applied to multinomial processing tree models within the context of cognitive storage and retrieval systems ( Chechile, 2004 ). Other Bayesian approaches include constrained latent class analysis ( Hoijtink, 1998 ) and inequality constrained analysis of variance ( Klugkist, Laudy, & Hoijtink, 2005 ). Johnson & Albert (1999) give a detailed overview of both Bayesian and classical methodologies for ordinal regression models.  Knapp and Batachelder (2004)  detail a methodology to re-parameterize a class of order constrained multinomial processing tree models (MPT) into statistically equivalent ones that are unconstrained, thus enabling a proper analysis.  Hu and Batchelder (2007)  generalize these results to other types of multinomial processing tree models and constraints.

This article is organized as follows: I first present the canonical form of the multinomial likelihood and linear inequality-defined model along with several relevant properties. The next section casts maximum likelihood estimation in terms of a standard convex optimization routine. This is followed by a necessary approximation of the inequality-constrained model. The subsequent section applies a series of results from the constrained inference literature to derive the asymptotic distribution of the likelihood ratio test statistic. This method is then applied in more general settings, namely when dealing with unions of “convex polytopes”. The second half of the article demonstrates how this methodology can be used to evaluate properly reformulated measurement axioms. This section is self-contained, in that it is an application of the methodology to existing reformulations of measurement axioms. Lastly, I give a discussion of the local nature of this test along with limitations of the parametric bootstrap under linear inequality constraints.

Theorems 2–4  are known results in the constrained inference literature and are stated without proof.  Lemmas 1–3  and  Theorem 1  are necessary results to apply the existing methodology to the case of jointly independent multinomial random variables of varying categorical size.

Note that the categorical size  
                         
                            ( 
                            
                               
                                  p 
                               
                               
                                  j 
                               
                            
                            ) 
                         
                       or number ( 
                         n 
                      ) of the multinomial random variables is not random, but is determined by the nature of the experiment. As an illustration, consider a two-alternative forced-choice experiment. Suppose a subject is presented with two choice alternatives and must choose either one or the other and that this same pair is shown repeatedly. The observable data for each individual trial in this experiment is a binary outcome. Assuming independence, each trial can be considered as a Bernoulli trial, giving rise to a binomial distribution as the statistical model across repeated  
                         i 
                         . 
                         i 
                         . 
                         d 
                      . observations. If there are more than two alternatives in the experiment, say three, then assuming independence between pairs, all paired comparisons can be modeled jointly with a product of independent binomial distributions. For this example, all paired comparisons of the choice alternatives could be modeled with  
                         
                            
                               3 
                               ∗ 
                               2 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                         = 
                         3 
                       many binomial distributions. Then under the general model presented above, we have  
                         n 
                         = 
                         3 
                       and

                               p 
                            
                            
                               3

                         1 
                       for this experiment. If each paired comparison is presented, say 10 times, then

Lemma 1 
                         
                            Assume the  
                            
                               log 
                            
                            -likelihood function in 
                            (2) 
                            . The log-likelihood is a unimodal, strictly concave function of  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                             
                            .

Assume the  
                            
                               log 
                            
                            -likelihood function in 
                            (2) 
                            . The log-likelihood is a unimodal, strictly concave function of  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                             
                            .

Proof 
                         To show unimodality, first note that the first partial derivative of  
                               l 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                             with respect to  
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     i 
                                     , 
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                             is  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           ∂ 
                                           l 
                                           
                                              ( 
                                              
                                                 θ 
                                              
                                              ) 
                                           
                                        
                                        
                                           ∂ 
                                           
                                              
                                                 θ 
                                              
                                              
                                                 i 
                                                 , 
                                                 j 
                                              
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                     = 
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              
                                                 m 
                                              
                                              
                                                 i 
                                                 , 
                                                 j 
                                              
                                           
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                              
                                                 θ 
                                              
                                              
                                                 i 
                                                 , 
                                                 j 
                                              
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                     − 
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              
                                                 N 
                                              
                                              
                                                 j 
                                              
                                           
                                           − 
                                           
                                              
                                                 ∑ 
                                              
                                              
                                                 i 
                                                 = 
                                                 1 
                                              
                                              
                                                 
                                                    
                                                       p 
                                                    
                                                    
                                                       j 
                                                    
                                                 
                                              
                                           
                                           
                                              
                                                 m 
                                              
                                              
                                                 i 
                                                 , 
                                                 j 
                                              
                                           
                                        
                                        
                                           1 
                                           − 
                                           
                                              
                                                 ∑ 
                                              
                                              
                                                 i 
                                                 = 
                                                 1 
                                              
                                              
                                                 
                                                    
                                                       p 
                                                    
                                                    
                                                       j 
                                                    
                                                 
                                              
                                           
                                           
                                              
                                                 θ 
                                              
                                              
                                                 i 
                                                 , 
                                                 j 
                                              
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                     . 
                                  
                               
                             If we set the first partial derivative of  
                               l 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                             equal to zero and solve the resulting systems of equations for all  
                               i 
                               , 
                               j 
                            , we obtain  
                               
                                  q 
                               
                             as the unique solution. This is because solving the above system of equations for all  
                               i 
                               , 
                               j 
                             is equivalent to solving the  
                               n 
                            -many systems of equations for each individual multinomial random variable  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                            , in  
                               M 
                            . 
                         Next we consider strict concavity. It is a well-known property that the matrix of second derivatives (Hessian matrix) of the log-likelihood function for a single multinomial random variable (i.e., when  
                               n 
                               = 
                               1 
                            ) is negative definite ( Hocking & Oxspring, 1971 ), which implies that the log-likelihood is a strictly concave function of  
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                            . The same is true for the case when  
                               n 
                               ≥ 
                               1 
                            . Let  
                               
                                  H 
                               
                             be the Hessian matrix for  
                               l 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                            . Let  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        H 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                             be the Hessian matrix for the log-likelihood corresponding to  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                            . Since  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           ∂ 
                                        
                                        
                                           2 
                                        
                                     
                                     l 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        
                                           θ 
                                        
                                        ) 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     ∂ 
                                     
                                        
                                           θ 
                                        
                                        
                                           i 
                                           , 
                                           j 
                                        
                                     
                                     ∂ 
                                     
                                        
                                           θ 
                                        
                                        
                                           k 
                                           , 
                                           h 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               0 
                               , 
                               ∀ 
                               h 
                               ≠ 
                               j 
                            ,  
                               
                                  H 
                               
                             can be written as a block diagonal matrix composed of the Hessian matrices for each of the  
                               n 
                             multinomial log-likelihoods.  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        H 
                                     
                                     = 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        
                                           
                                              
                                                 
                                                    
                                                       
                                                          H 
                                                       
                                                    
                                                    
                                                       1 
                                                    
                                                 
                                              
                                              
                                                 0 
                                              
                                              
                                                 0 
                                              
                                              
                                                 … 
                                              
                                              
                                                 0 
                                              
                                           
                                           
                                              
                                                 0 
                                              
                                              
                                                 
                                                    
                                                       
                                                          H 
                                                       
                                                    
                                                    
                                                       2 
                                                    
                                                 
                                              
                                              
                                                 0 
                                              
                                              
                                                 … 
                                              
                                              
                                                 0 
                                              
                                           
                                           
                                              
                                                 ⋮ 
                                              
                                              
                                                 ⋮ 
                                              
                                              
                                                 ⋮ 
                                              
                                              
                                                 ⋱ 
                                              
                                              
                                                 ⋮ 
                                              
                                           
                                           
                                              
                                                 0 
                                              
                                              
                                                 … 
                                              
                                              
                                                 0 
                                              
                                              
                                                 0 
                                              
                                              
                                                 
                                                    
                                                       
                                                          H 
                                                       
                                                    
                                                    
                                                       n 
                                                    
                                                 
                                              
                                           
                                        
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     . 
                                  
                               
                            Each  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        H 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                             is a negative definite matrix for all values of  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                            , which is equivalent to the condition that all eigenvalues of  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        H 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                             are negative for all values of  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                            . Since  
                               det 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                               = 
                               det 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           H 
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        1 
                                     
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                               det 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           H 
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        2 
                                     
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                               ⋯ 
                               det 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           H 
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        n 
                                     
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                            , the characteristic polynomial of the matrix  
                               
                                  H 
                               
                             is the product of the characteristic polynomials of the component matrices  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        H 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                            ,  
                               j 
                               ∈ 
                               
                                  { 
                                  1 
                                  , 
                                  2 
                                  , 
                                  … 
                                  , 
                                  n 
                                  } 
                               
                            . Therefore, the eigenvalues of the matrix  
                               
                                  H 
                               
                             are the collection of all eigenvalues of the component matrices  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        H 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                            ,  
                               j 
                               ∈ 
                               
                                  { 
                                  1 
                                  , 
                                  2 
                                  , 
                                  … 
                                  , 
                                  n 
                                  } 
                               
                            . Hence all eigenvalues of  
                               
                                  H 
                               
                             are negative for all values of  
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                            , and thus  
                               
                                  H 
                               
                             is negative definite, which implies that  
                               l 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                             is strictly concave. □

Example 1A 
                         As a simple example, consider a likelihood of the form  (1)  with  
                               n 
                               = 
                               2 
                            ,  
                               
                                  
                                     p 
                                  
                                  
                                     1 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               1 
                            , and  
                               
                                  
                                     p 
                                  
                                  
                                     2 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               1 
                            . This gives  
                               M 
                             equal to a vector of two independent binomial random variables. Also, let  
                               
                                  
                                     N 
                                  
                                  
                                     1 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               10 
                               , 
                               
                                  
                                     N 
                                  
                                  
                                     2 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               10 
                            , and suppose we observe  
                               
                                  
                                     m 
                                  
                                  
                                     1 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               5 
                               , 
                               
                                  
                                     m 
                                  
                                  
                                     2 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               6 
                            . This gives the following form of the joint multinomial likelihood

This definition of a substantive model includes the case of equality restrictions as a special case, because  
                         
                            
                               
                                  g 
                               
                            
                            
                               
                                  T

                         = 
                         c

                         ≤ 
                         c 
                         ∧ 
                         − 
                         
                            
                               
                                  g 
                               
                            
                            
                               
                                  T 
                               
                            
                         
                         
                            x 
                         
                         ≤ 
                         − 
                         c 
                      .

The restrictions comprising the set  
                         G 
                       may be quite simple. For example, if the parameter in a single binomial random variable is constrained to be  
                         
                            
                               1 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                      , this can be used to test whether a coin is “fair”. A more elaborate example consists of determining whether a subject’s preferences over a finite set of objects is transitive.  Regenwetter and Davis-Stober (2008)  review several different multinomial models for testing transitivity of preferences.

It is useful to introduce additional geometric structure to describe the set

                         A  face  of a convex polytope  
                               P 
                             is defined as the intersection of  
                               P 
                             with a hyperplane, such that  
                               P 
                             is entirely contained in one of the two half-spaces determined by that hyperplane. The polytope  
                               P 
                             itself is a face, all faces that do not equal  
                               P 
                             are called proper faces. A proper face of maximal dimension is called a  facet . A convex polytope is compactly described by its collection of  facet-defining inequalities , i.e., the shortest possible list of linear inequalities such that the solution set is that convex polytope. Lemma 2 
                         
                            Let the likelihood function be defined as in 
                            (1) 
                            . Let  
                            
                               G 
                             
                            and  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     Θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                               
                             
                            be defined as in 
                            (2) 
                            . Assume that the set  
                            
                               G 
                             
                            is nonempty. Then  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     Θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                               
                             
                            is a convex polytope.

Let the likelihood function be defined as in 
                            (1) 
                            . Let  
                            
                               G 
                             
                            and  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     Θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                               
                             
                            be defined as in 
                            (2) 
                            . Assume that the set  
                            
                               G 
                             
                            is nonempty. Then  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     Θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                               
                             
                            is a convex polytope.

Proof 
                         Assume that the space  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        0 
                                        , 
                                        1 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     p 
                                  
                               
                             can be closed as necessary by adding a set of Lebesgue measure 0. First, each linear inequality in  
                               G 
                             defines a closed half-space, i.e., the solution space to each inequality in  
                               G 
                             is a half-space. Geometrically, the solution space to the conjunction of all inequalities in  
                               G 
                             is the intersection of all corresponding half-spaces. By definition,  
                               
                                  
                                     Θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                               
                             is bounded by the unit cube. Hence,  
                               
                                  
                                     Θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                               
                             is a bounded intersection of a finite collection of half-spaces, which is by definition a convex polytope. □

Assume that the space

                               G 
                             defines a closed half-space, i.e., the solution space to each inequality in  
                               G 
                             is a half-space. Geometrically, the solution space to the conjunction of all inequalities in  
                               G 
                             is the intersection of all corresponding half-spaces. By definition,  
                               
                                  
                                     Θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                               
                             is bounded by the unit cube. Hence,  
                               
                                  
                                     Θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                               
                             is a bounded intersection of a finite collection of half-spaces, which is by definition a convex polytope. □

In this section, I present properties of the likelihood function  (1)  relevant to maximum likelihood estimation under the substantive model. I also introduce a geometric structure that greatly simplifies the constrained estimation process.                          ϵ

                                  ( 
                                  
                                     x 
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                               ≠ 
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            , then  
                               − 
                               ∇

                                     x 
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                             defines a supporting hyperplane to  
                               X

                                  x 
                               
                             ( Boyd & Vandenberghe, 2004 ). By assumption,  
                               
                                  q

                               − 
                               ∇

                             at  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     ˆ 
                                  
                               
                            . Therefore,  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     ˆ 
                                  
                               
                             lies on the boundary of  
                               
                                  
                                     Θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                               
                            . Uniqueness follows from strict concavity, i.e., a strictly concave differentiable function over a convex set can have at most one optimal point ( Boyd & Vandenberghe, 2004 ). □

Proof 
                         By  Theorem 1 , the maximum likelihood estimate is unique and lies on the boundary of  
                               
                                  
                                     Θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                               
                            . The boundary of any polytope is the the union of all proper faces, and any nonempty intersection of two faces is again a face. Hence the point  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     ˆ 
                                  
                               
                             lies on a face of the convex polytope  
                               
                                  
                                     Θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                               
                            . □

The following results depend upon the asymptotic distribution of the maximum likelihood estimate. Of course, experimentally we are forced to deal with finite data.  Robertson et al. (1988)  suggested that the usual condition of

                            5 
                         , should be sufficient for the binomial case under simple order constraints. However, it is not known what the minimum sample size should be under more general constraints, although individual cases could be estimated using Monte Carlo methods.

                            =

The set of hypotheses when the null hypothesis is contained within a cone is often referred to as a “Type B” testing problem in the constrained inference literature ( Silvapulle & Sen, 2005 ). A hypothesis test of this form has an alternative description in terms of the polar representation of the approximating cone.

Theorem 4 
                            
                               Shapiro, 1985

Assume that the true value of

Estimating the weights  
                            
                               
                                  w 
                               
                               
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     i 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                            
                          of  (11)  is difficult. Early efforts by a number of statisticians produced weights for many interesting constraints ( Barlow, Bartholomew, Bremner, & Brunk, 1972; Robertson et al., 1988 ), but a general solution was not known. More recently, the weights in  (13)  have been shown to be functions of a geometric property in polyhedral cones known as “mixed volumes” ( Takemura & Kuriki, 1997 ).  Lin and Lindsay (1997)  have also solved for the weights for a related class of cone constraints, not limited to linear constraints, using an application of Wely’s tube volume formula from differential geometry. However, both of these solutions gives the weights as complicated integrals that are not simple to evaluate.  El Barmi and Dykstra (1995)  have also derived analytic solutions to the above weights for a class of multinomial models under a set of polyhedral cone constraints. Fortunately, recent work has led to a more general simulation-based method for obtaining stable estimates of the weights when the hypothesis test involves polyhedral cone constraints ( Silvapulle, 1996 ).

Intuitively, the Silvapulle algorithm estimates the weights of the  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        χ 
                                     
                                     ¯ 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  2

                                     θ 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ˆ

                                  C 
                               
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                     
                                        ˆ

                            d 
                         , which is strictly less than the dimensionality of the cone itself (e.g., a cube has 1 and 2-dimensional faces corresponding to ‘edges’ and ‘sides’). The dimensionality of each face directly corresponds to the degrees of freedom in the resulting  
                            
                               
                                  χ 
                               
                               
                                  2

                                  2

                            d

                                  2 
                               
                            
                          in  (13)  means that a sampled point has probability  
                            
                               
                                  1 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                          of being projected onto a face of dimension 2.

Over the last century, axiomatic measurement theory has played a major role in defining how mathematical models of human behavior are constructed. Measurement axioms have been the foundation for decision theories of human behavior such as expected utility theory ( von Neumann & Morgenstern, 1947 ), discounted utility theory ( Koopman, 1960 ), and prospect theory ( Kahneman & Tversky, 1979 ). A complete axiomatic description is a set of mathematical statements that are both necessary and sufficient for a specific formal representation to exist ( Krantz, Luce, Suppes, & Tversky, 1971 ). If these statements are satisfied, then there exists a homomorphism between the empirical structure of interest (e.g., gambles) and a real-valued mathematical structure (e.g., the real number line with the usual definitions of “+” and “ ×”).

Luce and Narens (1994)  described the problem of determining whether such axioms hold empirically as one of the major unsolved problems in axiomatic measurement theory. The core difficulty in evaluating axioms is that they are defined algebraically, therefore deterministically. However, experimental data are the outcomes of sampling processes, this implies that a probabilistic reformulation of an axiom is necessary to apply statistical analysis. In this article, the assumption is made that preferences are being modeled in a systematically nondeterministic way ( Loomes & Sugden, 1995 ). This is in contrast to models that assume preferences to be deterministic and add a “noise” component to model choice, such as Thurstonian and Fechnerian scaling. Recently,  Tsai and Böckenholt (2006)  have tested the axiom of transitivity using a random effects statistical framework under Thurstonian modeling assumptions.

The challenge of testing an algebraic axiom is twofold. First, a suitable probabilistic reformulation of the axiom is needed to account for sampling variability. Typically, a probabilistic reformulation of an axiom constrains a set of probabilities (e.g., binary choice probabilities) to lie in a union of convex polytopes ( Iverson & Falmagne, 1985 ). This fact leads to the second part of the axiom testing challenge, namely utilizing a proper statistical test to carry out the analysis. Attempts to solve the second part of this challenge have fallen along the two major statistical schools of thought: “classical” and Bayesian.

Iverson and Falmagne (1985)  pioneered the “classical” approach to the statistical analysis of reformulated measurement axioms by modeling paired comparisons with independent binomial random variables and determining goodness of fit with a likelihood ratio test. Under this framework, the probabilistic axiom is treated as a null hypothesis to be overturned. However, due to the inequality restrictions for the reformulated axioms they considered, the asymptotic distribution of the likelihood ratio test statistic was not  
                         
                            
                               χ 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                      .  Iverson and Falmagne (1985)  were able to derive a proper  
                         α 
                      -level test for a handful of reformulated axioms, but were unable to generalize the technique further, due to theoretical and computational limitations at the time.  Iverson and Harp (1987)  later developed a conditional likelihood ratio test to approximate the proper distribution under inequality restrictions.  Suck (1997)  also investigated a variant of this method for the bi-classification problem.

Most recently,  Myung et al. (2005) , have introduced a Bayesian methodology to analyze reformulated algebraic axioms. The Bayesian method departs from the classical perspective by no longer considering the reformulated axiom as a null hypothesis to be overturned. Rather, a collection of reformulated axioms are specified, with the best fitting model according to the Bayesian deviance information criterion (DIC) selected as the best description of the data. This methodology is general in the sense that nonnested models can be compared with one another.

The work described in this article represents a generalization of the classical perspective on axiom testing first developed by  Iverson and Falmagne (1985) . To illustrate this methodology, we present three examples drawn from the axiomatic measurement literature. First, we consider testing whether binary choices are induced by linear rankings using a subset of the well known  Tversky (1969)  data set. Second, we test whether ternary choices are induced by weak orders. Finally, we apply these techniques to the analysis of multiple choice induced by rankings.

Let  
                         K 
                       denote a collection of choice alternatives. The problem of determining whether binary choices are induced by linear orders over the set  
                         K

See  Marley (1990)  for a detailed review of earlier work on this topic.

Definition 
                         Let  
                               K

Let

Theorem 
                         
                            Fiorini & Fishburn, 2004 
                         
                         
                            Let  
                            
                               
                                  | 
                                  K 
                                  | 
                               
                             
                            be finite. Ternary paired comparison probabilities on  
                            
                               K 
                             
                            are induced by weak orders if and only if they are contained in the weak order polytope.

Let  
                            
                               
                                  | 
                                  K 
                                  | 
                               
                             
                            be finite. Ternary paired comparison probabilities on  
                            
                               K 
                             
                            are induced by weak orders if and only if they are contained in the weak order polytope.

The facet structure for the weak order polytope is considerably more complicated than the linear order polytope. See  Fiorini (2001b) ,  Fiorini and Fishburn (2004) , and  Regenwetter and Davis-Stober (2008)  for complete linear descriptions of the weak order polytope for  
                         
                            | 
                            K 
                            | 
                         
                         ≤ 
                         4 
                      .

As an example, consider data from  Regenwetter and Davis-Stober (2008) , which details a choice experiment similar to the  Tversky (1969)  study described above, with the exception that subjects were allowed to express indifference. More precisely, subjects were presented with multiple repetitions of binary gamble pairs and were allowed the option of choosing indifference between them in lieu of selecting a preferred gamble. Fiorini (2003)  provided an alternative proof to the above result and commented that the above equations are the facet-defining inequalities to the  multiple-choice polytope . Hence,  (30) and (31)  state necessary and sufficient conditions to test whether multiple choice probabilities are, in fact, induced by rankings. (The set of equations given by  (30)  are also known as the Block–Marschak inequalities ( Falmagne, 1978 ).)

In this article I have summarized and extended previous work in the constrained inference literature to perform an  
                         α 
                      -level hypothesis test for independent multinomial random variables of differing categorical size, when the null model is constrained to be a (union of) convex polytope(s). This methodology has a natural application to axiomatic measurement theory as a means of evaluating properly reformulated measurement axioms.

This article describes a method to obtain the limiting distribution of the  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        χ 
                                     
                                     ¯ 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                          distribution conditional on the location of  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ˆ 
                               
                            
                         . This approach is in many ways analogous to  Iverson and Harp ’s ( 1987 ) conditional likelihood ratio test. As a result, the asymptotic distribution of  
                            
                               
                                  Λ 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                          may have a different  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        χ 
                                     
                                     ¯ 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                          distribution depending on which face of  
                            
                               
                                  Θ 
                               
                               
                                  G 
                               
                            
                          contains  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ˆ 
                               
                            
                         . Unfortunately, for most polytopes there is no guarantee that the distribution at a given value of  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ˆ 
                               
                            
                          is the least favorable under  
                            
                               
                                  Θ 
                               
                               
                                  G 
                               
                            
                         .

However, a least favorable distribution will typically be an overly conservative test, with  
                            α 
                          as only an upper bound of Type I error, thus potentially leading to a statistical test with low power. Consequently, in a related context of examining multinomial models under polyhedral cone constraints,  El Barmi and Dykstra (1995)  recommend basing the hypothesis test on the distribution derived from the observed maximum likelihood value, in agreement with the method developed in this article. El Barmi and Dykstra reached this conclusion after numerous simulations. Simulations of the method described here agree with this conclusion.

An unconditional variant of this test is possible. An investigator could sample parameter values over the boundary of the constrained model, computing a distribution for each value and collecting them into a set. Once all distributions of this sample have been enumerated, the observed value of  
                            
                               
                                  Λ 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                          is tested against the most conservative distribution in this set of estimated distributions. This approach gives an estimate of the least favorable distribution under  
                            
                               
                                  Θ 
                               
                               
                                  G 
                               
                            
                          and minimizes Type I error. However, there is no way to know just how conservative this test would be, or if the most conservative distribution in the sample were truly the most conservative overall.

In situations where a closed-form solution of the distribution of a test statistic can be difficult or impossible to obtain, a common strategy is to simulate the distribution of interest by using a parametric bootstrap ( Efron, 1979 ). However, the parametric bootstrap may be an inconsistent estimate of the true distribution when the parameter lies on the boundary of the permissible parameter space ( Andrews, 2000; Geyer, 1995 ). Thus, there is no guarantee that the distribution obtained using a parametric bootstrap is asymptotically correct. The inconsistency of the parametric bootstrap in this situation is due to the lack of continuity near the boundary of the parameter space, see  Geyer (1995)  for a thorough treatment. Both  Geyer (1995)  and  Andrews (2000)  present modifications to the parametric bootstrapping algorithm to attain the correct asymptotic behavior.

It has been assumed throughout this article that the inequalities comprising the set  
                         G 
                       are the facet-defining inequalities of a (union of) convex polytope(s). This can be a limiting factor, especially when working with order polytopes defined on a large value of  
                         
                            | 
                            K 
                            | 
                         
                      . For example, determining a complete linear description of several order polytopes (e.g., linear, parital, weak), is NP-Complete. Thus, providing a complete linear description of the order polytope when the set of choice alternatives is large is at this time computationally infeasible. Additional work is needed to generalize the results discussed in this article to convex polytopes where a full linear description is not available.
